                                                   A BSTRACT
014
015
               This paper aims to clearly distinguish between Stochastic Gradient Descent with
               Momentum (SGDM) and Adam in terms of their convergence rates. We demon-
016
               strate that Adam achieves a faster convergence compared to SGDM under the
017
               condition of non-uniformly bounded smoothness. Our findings reveal that: (1)
018            in deterministic environments, Adam can attain the known lower bound for the
019            convergence rate of deterministic first-order optimizers, whereas the convergence
020            rate of Gradient Descent with Momentum (GDM) has higher order dependence
021            on the initial function value; (2) in stochastic setting, AdamвҖҷs convergence rate
022            upper bound matches the lower bounds of stochastic first-order optimizers, consid-
023            ering both the initial function value and the final error, whereas there are instances
024            where SGDM fails to converge with any learning rate. These insights distinctly
025            differentiate Adam and SGDM regarding their convergence rates. Additionally,
026
               by introducing a novel stopping-time based technique, we further prove that if
               we consider the minimum gradient norm during iterations, the corresponding con-
027
               vergence rate can match the lower bounds across all problem hyperparameters.
028
               The technique can also help proving that Adam with a specific hyperparameter
029            scheduler is parameter-agnostic, which hence can be of independent interest.
030
031
032   1    I NTRODUCTION
033
034   Among various optimization techniques, the Adam optimizer Kingma & Ba (2014); Loshchilov &
035   Hutter (2019) stands out due to its empirical success in a wide range of deep learning applications,
036   especially for pre-training large foundation models with enormous data Touvron et al. (2023); Brown
037   et al. (2020); Zhang et al. (2022a); Rae et al. (2021); Chowdhery et al. (2022); Du et al. (2021).
038   This popularity of Adam can be attributed to its adaptive learning rate mechanism, which smartly
039
      adjusts the step size for each parameter, allowing flexible and robust learning rate choices. AdamвҖҷs
      versatility is further highlighted by its consistent performance in training various kinds of models,
040
      making it a preferred optimizer in both academic and industrial settings Schneider et al. (2022).
041
      Its empirical success extends beyond standard benchmarks to real-world challenges, where it often
042   delivers state-of-the-art results. This track record solidifies AdamвҖҷs position as a fundamental tool for
043   deep learning practitioners.
044
045
      Exploring the theoretical foundations of the Adam optimizer, particularly why it often outperforms
      traditional optimizers like Stochastic Gradient Descent with Momentum (SGDM), is an intriguing
046
      yet complex task. Understanding AdamвҖҷs convergence behavior is challenging, especially in settings
047
      defined by standard convergence rate analysis. In these settings, assumptions include uniformly
048   bounded smoothness and finite gradient noise variance. Current research indicates that under these
049   conditions, SGDM can attain the lower bound of the convergence rate for all first-order optimizers
050   Carmon et al. (2017). This finding implies that, theoretically, AdamвҖҷs convergence rate should not
051   exceed that of SGDM. This theoretical result contrasts with practical observations where Adam
052   frequently excels, presenting a fascinating challenge for researchers. It highlights the need for
053   more refined theoretical models that can bridge the gap between AdamвҖҷs empirical success and its
      theoretical understanding.


                                                         1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      Recent research by Zhang et al. (2019) has provided valuable insights into the complexity of neural
055   network optimization, particularly challenging the assumption of uniform bounded smoothness. Their
056   observations indicate that smoothness often varies, showing a positive correlation with the norm of
057   the gradient and experiencing considerable fluctuations during the optimization process. Building on
058   this, they introduce the (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition (detailed in our Assumption 1), which posits that
059   local smoothness can be bounded in relation to the gradient norm. This concept presents an exciting
060   opportunity to theoretically demonstrate that Adam could potentially converge faster than SGDM.
061   However, even in the relatively simpler deterministic settings, no study has yet conclusively shown
062   this to be the case.
063   To effectively compare the convergence rates of Adam and Stochastic Gradient Descent with Mo-
064   mentum (SGDM), itвҖҷs essential to establish an upper bound on AdamвҖҷs convergence rate and a lower
065   bound for SGDM, and then prove AdamвҖҷs superiority. This endeavor faces several challenges. First,
066   the known lower bound for SGDMвҖҷs convergence rate is only available in deterministic settings
067   without momentum Zhang et al. (2019); Crawshaw et al. (2022). Moreover, this result is based on a
068   scenario where the counter-example objective function is selected after fixing the learning rate. This
069
      procedure deviates from more common practices where the learning rate is adjusted after defining
      the objective function Drori & Shamir (2020); Carmon et al. (2017); Arjevani et al. (2022), casting
070
      doubts on the standard applicability of this lower bound. Secondly, for Adam, the current assumptions
071
      required to derive an upper bound for its convergence rate are quite strict. These include assumptions
072   like bounded adaptive learning rates or deterministically bounded noise Wang et al. (2022); Li et al.
073   (2023a). However, even under these constraints, the convergence rates obtained for Adam are weaker
074   than those of algorithms like clipped SGDM Zhang et al. (2019).
075
      These complexities hinder a straightforward comparison between the convergence rates of Adam and
076
      SGDM, highlighting a significant gap in the theoretical understanding that remains to be bridged.
077
078   Our contributions. In this paper, we aim to bridge the gap and summarize our contributions as
079   follows.
080         вҖў We separate the convergence rate of Adam and SGDM under (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition
081            both in the deterministic setting and in the stochastic setting.
082               вҖ“ In the deterministic setting, for the first time, we prove that under the (L0 , L1 )-smooth
083                 condition, the convergence rate of the Adam optimizer can match the existing lower
084                 bound for first-order deterministic optimizers, up to numerical constants. Additionally,
085                 we establish a new lower bound for the convergence rate of GDM, where one is allowed
086                 to tune the learning rate and the momentum coefficient after the problem is fixed.
087
                    The lower bound exhibits a higher order dependence on the initial function value gap
                    compared to the upper bound of Adam. This distinction clearly separates Adam and
088
                    GDM for the deterministic setting.
089
                  вҖ“ In the stochastic setting, for the first time, we prove that under the (L0 , L1 )-smooth
090
                    condition, the convergence rate of Adam matches the existing lower bound for first-
091
                    order stochastic optimizers regarding the initial function value f (w1 ) вҲ’ f вҲ— and the final
092                 error Оө. In contrast, counterexamples exist where SGDM fails to converge, irrespective
093                 of the learning rate and momentum coefficient. These findings distinctly separate the
094                 convergence properties of Adam and SGDM in stochastic settings.
095          вҖў With the aid of a novel stopping time based technique, we further demonstrate that the
096            convergence rate of minimum error point of Adam can match the lower bound across all
097            problem hyperparameters. We demonstrate that such a technique can be of independent
098            interest by proving that Adam with specific scheduler is parameter-agnostic based on the
099            stopping time.
100
101   2   R ELATED W ORKS
102
103   Convergence analysis under non-uniform smoothness. Observations from empirical studies
104   on deep neural network training indicate that local smoothness can vary significantly throughout
105   the optimization process. In response to this, Zhang et al. (2019) introduced the (L0 , L1 )-smooth
106   condition, which posits that local smoothness can be bounded by a linear function of the gradient norm.
107   Subsequent works have extended this concept by generalizing the linear function to polynomials Chen
      et al. (2023); Li et al. (2023a), or to more general functions Mei et al. (2021). Under non-uniform


                                                         2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      smoothness, convergence properties of various optimizers have been studied. For instance, upper
109
      bounds on the convergence rate have been established for optimizers such as Clipped SGDM Zhang
110   et al. (2020), sign-based optimizers Jin et al. (2021); HuМҲbler et al. (2023); Sun et al. (2023), AdaGrad
111   Faw et al. (2023); Wang et al. (2023b), variance-reduction methods Reisizadeh et al. (2023); Chen
112   et al. (2023), and trust-region methods Xie et al. (2023). However, research on lower bounds has
113   been comparatively limited, with results primarily focusing on Gradient Descent.
114
      Convergence analysis of Adam. The development of convergence analysis for Adam has been quite
115
      tortuous. While Adam was originally proposed with a convergence guarantee Kingma & Ba (2014),
116   subsequent analysis by Reddi et al. (2018) pointed out flaws in this initial analysis and provided
117   counterexamples claiming that Adam could fail to converge. Only recently, Shi et al. (2021) and
118   Zhang et al. (2022b) have shown that the counterexamples in Reddi et al. (2018) only rule out the
119   possibility that Adam can converge problem-agnostically, and it is still possible that Adam can
120   converge with problem-dependent hyperparameters.
121
      So far, several works have established the convergence of Adam under the L-smooth condition.
122   Zaheer et al. (2018) proved that Adam without momentum can converge to the neighborhood of
123   stationary points by additionally assuming that О» is large. De et al. (2018) showed that Adam
124   without momentum can converge to stationary points but under the strong assumption that the sign of
125   gradients does not change during the optimization. Zou et al. (2019), DeМҒfossez et al. (2022), and Guo
126   et al. (2021) derived the convergence of Adam by assuming the stochastic gradient is bounded. Shi
127   et al. (2021) and Zhang et al. (2022b) characterized the convergence of random-reshuffling Adam but
128   suffer from sub-optimal rates. He et al. (2023) studied the non-ergodic convergence of Adam under
129   a bounded gradient assumption, while Hong & Lin (2023) provided high-probability guarantees
130
      for Adam under a deterministically bounded noise assumption. A concurrent work by Wang et al.
      (2023a) shows that Adam can achieve the lower bound of first-order optimizers with respect to the
131
      final error Оө under standard assumptions, but it is unknown whether Adam can match the lower bound
132
      with respect to other problem specifics.
133
134   On the other hand, closely related to our work, there are only two works studying the convergence
135   of Adam under non-uniform smoothness Wang et al. (2022); Li et al. (2023a), both with restricted
136
      assumptions and results. We will provide a detailed discussion in Section 4.
137

181   4     S EPARATING THE CONVERGENCE RATES OF A DAM AND (S)GD
182
183   In this section, we elucidate the disparate convergence rates of Adam and (S)GD under Assumptions
184   1 and 2, examining both deterministic and stochastic settings. We commence with the deterministic
185   scenario before delving into the stochastic complexities.
186
187   4.1   A NALYSIS FOR THE DETERMINISTIC SETTING
188
189   As discussed in the introduction section, to discern the differential convergence rates of deterministic
190   Adam and GD, it is necessary to establish not only AdamвҖҷs upper bound but also GDвҖҷs lower bound,
191   given a consistent set of assumptions. Crucially, these bounds must be sufficiently tight to ensure
192   that AdamвҖҷs upper bound is indeed the lesser. To date, only a couple of studies have addressed
193
      the convergence of deterministic Adam. The first, referenced in Wang et al. (2022), indicates a
                                           вҲ— 2

194   convergence rate of O( (f (w1Оө)вҲ’f2

196   The second study, Li et al. (2023a), presents a convergence rate that depends polynomially on О»1 ,
197   where О» is the small constant introduced to prevent the adaptive learning rate from becoming infinity.
198   Therefore, their result is only non-vacuous when О» is large, which deviates from practical settings.
199   Additionally, their bound exhibits an exaggerated dependency on the initial function value gap,

200   yielding mintвҲҲ[T ] вҲҘвҲҮf (wt )вҲҘ = O( (f (w1Оө)вҲ’f   2

225   draws inspiration from the current analysis of Gradient Descent with Momentum (GDM) under the
226   L-smooth condition Sun et al. (2019). However, we have introduced significant modifications to
227   accommodate the integration of an adaptive learning rate. This carefully crafted Lyapunov function

231   Remark 1 (On the comparison with AdaGrad). Our result also suffices to separate Adam from
232
      AdaGrad. It is important to note that the convergence rate of AdaGrad under the (L0 , L1 )-smooth
                                                                                               вҲ— 2
233   condition in a deterministic setting, as reported inWang et al. (2023b), is (f (w1Оө)вҲ’f
                                                                                           2
                                                                                                )
                                                                                                   . This rate
234   is outperformed by that of Adam1 . In Appendix B.3, we show that the rate in Wang et al. (2023b)
235   is tight by providing a counterexample. The comparatively slower convergence rate of AdaGrad
236   can be attributed to that (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition demands the update norm to be bounded by
237   O(1) to prevent the local smoothness from exponentially increasing. This, in turn, necessitates a
238
      learning rate of O(1). However, the adaptive conditioner in AdaGrad, which accumulates over time,
      causes the adaptive learning rate to become excessively small during later training stages, resulting
239
      in reduced convergence speed. Conversely, Adam utilizes an exponential moving average for its
240
      adaptive learning rate, which prevents the conditioner from accumulating excessively. Consequently,
241   Adam does not suffer from the aforementioned issue.
242
243   A lower bound for the convergence rate of GDM
244
      With AdamвҖҷs upper bound, we then move on to a lower bound for the convergence rate of GDM. In
245   fact, there has already been such lower bounds for GD in the existing literature Zhang et al. (2019);
246   Crawshaw et al. (2022), which we restate as follows:
247

      However, the proposition presents a limitation: the counter-example is chosen after the learning rate
255
      has been determined. This approach is inconsistent with standard practices, where hyperparameters
256   are usually adjusted based on the specific task, and deviates from conventional lower bounds Carmon
257   et al. (2017); Arjevani et al. (2022) that offer assurances for optimally-tuned hyperparameters. This
258   type of result does not eliminate the possibility that, if the learning rate were adjusted after selecting
259   the objective functionвҖ”as is common practiceвҖ”Gradient Descent (GD) could potentially achieve a
260   markedly faster convergence rate. This misalignment raises concerns about the appropriateness of
261   the propositionвҖҷs methodology. Moreover, this proposition does not take momentum into account, a
262   technique that is commonly employed in conjunction with GD in practice.
263   To address these shortcomings, we introduce a new lower bound for GDM. This lower bound is
264   applicable under the standard practice of adjusting hyperparameters after the objective function has
265   been selected. Moreover, it encompasses scenarios where momentum is incorporated.

278   It should be noticed in the above theorem, the hyperparameters (i.e., the learning rate and the
279   momentum coefficient) are chosen after the objective function is determined, which agrees with
280   practice and the settings of common lower bounds, and overcomes the shortcoming of Proposition

282   convergence rate is also O 1 1Оө2 0 1 , which indicates such a lower bound is optimal.
283
284   The proof addresses two primary challenges outlined above. The first challenge involves handling
285   momentum. To tackle this, we extend the counterexample provided in Proposition 1 for cases where
286
      the momentum coefficient ОІ is small. Additionally, we introduce a new counterexample for situations
      with a large ОІ, demonstrating how large momentum can bias the optimization process and decelerate
287
      convergence. The second challenge is how to derive a universal counterexample such that every
288
      hyperparameter setting will lead to slow convergence. We overcome this by a simple but effective
289   trick: we independently put counterexamples for different hyperparameters in Proposition 1 over
290   different coordinates and make it a whole counterexample. Therefore, for different hyperparameters,
291   there will be at least one coordinate converge slowly, which leads to the final result.
292
      Separating deterministic Adam and GDM. Upon careful examination of Theorem 1 and            Theorem 2,
293                                                                                              PT
                                                                                                      вҲҘG вҲҘ
294   it becomes apparent that the convergence rate of GDM is inferior to that of Adam since t=1T t вүҘ
295   mintвҲҲ[T ] вҲҘGt вҲҘ. Notably, GDM exhibits a more pronounced dependency on the initial function value
296
      gap in comparison to Adam. This implies that with a sufficiently poor initial point, the convergence of
      GDM can be significantly slower than that of Adam. The underlying reason for this disparity can be
297
      attributed to GDMвҖҷs inability to adeptly manage varying degrees of sharpness within the optimization
298
      landscape. Consequently, GDM necessitates a learning rate selection that is conservative, tailored to
299   the most adverse sharpness encounteredвҖ”often present during the initial optimization stages.

301
302
      4.2   A NALYSIS FOR THE STOCHASTIC SETTING
303
      Transitioning to the more complex stochastic setting, we extend our analysis beyond the deterministic
304
      framework. As with our previous approach, we start by reviewing the literature to determine if
305   the existing convergence rates for Adam under the (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition can delineate a clear
306   distinction between the convergence behaviors of Adam and Stochastic Gradient Descent with
307   Momentum (SGDM). In fact, the only two studies that delve into this problem are the ones we
308   discussed in Section 4.1, i.e., Wang et al. (2022); Li et al. (2023a). However, these results pertaining
309   to Adam are contingent upon rather stringent assumptions. Wang et al. (2022) postulates that
310   stochastic gradients not only conform to the (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition but are also limited to a
311   finite set of possibilities. These assumptions are more restrictive than merely assuming that the true
312   gradients satisfy the (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition, and such strong prerequisites are seldom employed
313
      outside of the analysis of variance-reduction algorithms. Meanwhile, Li et al. (2023a) aligns its
      findings on stochastic Adam with those on deterministic Adam, leading to a polynomial dependency
314
      on 1/О», which deviates from practical scenarios as discussed in Section 4.1. Furthermore, it presumes
315
      an a.s. bounded difference between stochastic gradients and true gradients, an assumption that closely
316   resembles the boundedness of stochastic gradients and is more limiting than the standard assumption
317   of bounded variance for stochastic gradients.
318
319
      These more restricted and non-standard assumptions cast challenges in establishing a lower bound
      for the convergence of SGDM in the relevant contexts, let alone attempting a comparison between
320
      SGDM and Adam. In addition to the fact that these upper bounds fail to facilitate a clear comparison
321
      between Adam and SGDM, there are also concerns regarding their convergence rates. Wang et al.
322                                                   вҲ— 2
      (2022) reports a convergence rate of (f (w1Оө)вҲ’f
                                                   8
                                                       )
                                                          , which has a higher-order dependence on the initial
323
                                                       (f (w1 )вҲ’f вҲ— )
      function value gap and the final error than the        Оө4       rate established for Clipped SGDM under


                                                         6
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


324
      the (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition Zhang et al. (2020)2 . Furthermore, Li et al. (2023a) indicates a con-
325                                 вҲ— 4

326
      vergence rate of O( (f (w1 )вҲ’f Оө)4 poly(1/О») ), which, aside from the previously mentioned dependency
      issues on 1/О», shows a significantly stronger dependence over the initial function value gap compared

      to the analysis of Clipped SGDM. This naturally leads to the question of whether such rates for Adam
328
      can be improved to match Clipped SGDM.
329
330   To tackle these obstacles, we present the following upper bound for Adam.
331   An upper bound for the convergence rate of Adam.

342   Below we include several discussions regarding Theorem 3. To begin with, one can immediately
343   observe that Theorem 3 only requires Assumptions 1 and 2, and the convergence rate with respect
                                                                      вҲ—
344   to the initial function value gap and the final error f (w1Оө4)вҲ’f matches that of Clipped SGDM Zhang
345   et al. (2020) even with a weaker noise assumption. Therefore, our result successfully mitigate these
346   barriers raised above. Indeed, to the
                                           best ofвҲ—our  knowledge, it is for the first time that an algorithm
347   is shown to converge with rate O f (w1Оө4)вҲ’f only requiring Assumptions 1 and 2, showcasing the
348   advantage of Adam.
349
350   We briefly sketch the proof here before moving on to the result of SGDM. Specifically, the proof
351
      is inspired by recent analysis of Adam under L-smooth condition Wang et al. (2023a), but several
      challenges arise during the proof:
352
353             вҖў The first challenge lies in the additional error introduced by the (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition.
354               We address this by demonstrating that the telescoping sum involving the auxiliary function
                  вҲҘG вҲҘ2
355               вҲҡ t , as employed in Wang et al. (2023a), can bound this additional error when the
                    ОҪtвҲ’1
356               adaptive learning rate is upper bounded. Although the adaptive learning rate in the Adam
357               algorithm is not inherently bounded, we establish that the deviation incurred by employing
358               a bounded surrogate adaptive learning rate is manageable;
359             вҖў The second challenge involves deriving the desired dependence on the initial function
360               value gap. Wang et al. (2023a) introduces two distinct proof strategies for bounding the
361               conditioner ОҪt and determining the final convergence rate. However, one strategy introduces
362               an additional logarithmic dependence on Оө, while the other exhibits sub-optimal dependence
363               on the initial function value gap. We propose a novel two-stage divide-and-conquer approach
364               to surmount this issue. In the first stage, we bound ОҪt effectively. Subsequently, we
365               leverage this bound within the original descent lemma to achieve the optimal dependence on
366
                  f (w1 ) вҲ’ f вҲ— .
367   Remark 2 (On the limitations). Although Theorem 3 addresses certain deficiencies identified in prior
368   studies Wang et al. (2022); Li et al. (2023a), it is not without its limitations. As noted by Arjevani et al.
369   (2022), the established lower bound for the convergence rate of first-order optimization algorithms
370   under the L0 -smooth condition with bounded noise variance (specifically, Пғ0 = Пғ0 and Пғ1 = 1 as
                                      (f (w1 )вҲ’f вҲ— )L0 Пғ02
371   stated in Assumption 2) is O(           Оө4           ). This sets a benchmark for the performance under
372   Assumptions 1 and 2. The upper bound of AdamвҖҷs convergence rate as presented in Theorem 3 falls
373   short when compared to this benchmark, exhibiting a weaker noise scale dependency (Пғ03 as opposed
374
      to Пғ02 ) and additional dependencies on L1 and Пғ1 .
375   To address these issues, we demonstrate in the subsequent section that by focusing on the convergence
376   of the minimum gradient norm, E mintвҲҲ[T ] вҲҘвҲҮf (wt )вҲҘ, we can attain an improved convergence rate

             While Zhang et al. (2020) also assumes an a.s. bounded gap between stochastic gradients and true gradients.

381   We now establish the lower bound of SGDM. This is, however, more challenging than the deterministic
382   case as to the best of our knowledge, there is no such a lower bound in existing literature (despite that
383   the lower bounds of GD Zhang et al. (2019); Crawshaw et al. (2022) naturally offer a lower bound
384   of SGD, which is considerably loose given the factor of 1/Оө2 ). Intuitively, stochasticity can make
385   the convergence of GDM even worse, as random fluctuations can inadvertently propel the iterations
386   towards regions characterized by high smoothness even with a good initialization. We formulate this
387
      insight into the following theorem.
388   A lower bound for the convergence rate of SGDM.
389   Theorem 4 (Informal). Fix L0 , L1 , and вҲҶ1 , there exists objective function f satisfying (L0 , L1 )-
390   smooth condition and f (w1 ) вҲ’ f вҲ— = вҲҶ1 , and a gradient noise oracle satisfying Assumption 2, such
391   that for any learning rate О· > 0 and ОІ вҲҲ [0, 1], for all T > 0,
392

393                                 tвҲҲ[T ]
394
395   Proof. Please see Appendix C.2 for the formal statement of theorem and the proof.
396
      Theorem 4 provides concrete evidence for the challenges inherent in the convergence of SGDM. It
397   shows that there are instances that comply with Assumption 1 and Assumption 2 for which SGDM
398   fails to converge, regardless of the chosen learning rate and momentum coefficient. This outcome
399   confirms our earlier hypothesis: the stochastic elements within SGDM can indeed adversely affect its
400   convergence properties under non-uniform smoothness.
401
      Our proof is founded upon a pivotal observation: an objective function that escalates rapidly can
402
      effectively convert non-heavy-tailed noise into a вҖқheavy-tailedвҖқ one. In particular, under the (L0 , L1 )-
403   smooth condition, the magnitude of the gradient is capable of exponential growth. As a result, even
404   if the density diminishes exponentially, the expected value of the gradient norm may still become
405   unbounded. This situation mirrors what occurs under the L-smooth condition when faced with
406   heavy-tailed noise. Such a dynamic can lead to the non-convergence of SGDM.
407
      Separating Adam and SGDM. Considering that Adam can achieve convergence under Assumptions
408
      1 and 2, while SGD cannot, the superiority of Adam over SGDM becomes evident. It is important to
409   note, however, a recent study by Li et al. (2023b), which demonstrates that SGD can converge with
410   high probability under the same assumptions, provided the noise variance is bounded. We would like
411   to contextualize this finding in relation to our work as follows: First, this result does not conflict with
412   our Theorem 4, since our theorem pertains to bounds in expectation rather than with high probability.
413   Second, our comparison of Adam and SGDM within an in-expectation framework is reasonable and
414   aligns with the convention of most existing lower bounds in the literature Carmon et al. (2017); Drori
415   & Shamir (2020); Arjevani et al. (2022). Moreover, establishing high-probability lower bounds is
416   technically challenging, and there are few references to such bounds in the existing literature. Lastly,
417
      while we have not derived a corresponding high-probability lower bound for SGD, the upper bound
                                                     вҲ— 4
418   provided by Li et al. (2023b) is O( (f (w1Оө)вҲ’f
                                                  4
                                                      )
                                                         ), which indicates a less favorable dependency on the
419   initial function value gap compared to the bound for Adam.
420
421   5    C AN A DAM REACH THE LOWER BOUND OF THE CONVERGENCE RATE
422        UNDER (L0 , L1 )- SMOOTH CONDITION ?

424   As we mentioned in Remark 2, although Theorem 3 matches the lower bound established by Arjevani
425   et al. (2022) with respect to the initial function value gap f (w1 ) вҲ’ f вҲ— , the final error Оө, and the
426   smoothness coefficient L0 , it exhibits sub-optimal dependence on the noise scale Пғ0 and additional
427   dependence on L1 and Пғ1 . One may wonder whether these dependencies are inherently unavoidable
428   or if they stem from technical limitations in our analysis.
429
      Upon revisiting the proof, we identified that the sub-optimal dependencies arise from our strategy of
430   substituting the original adaptive learning rate with a bounded surrogate. For example, the correlation

474   One can easily see that the convergence rate of Theorem 5 matches the lower bound in Arjevani et al.
475   (2022) with respect to all problem hyperparameters up to numerical constants even under the weaker
476   (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition. Therefore, such a rate is optimal and provides an affirmative answer to
477   the question raised in the beginning of this section.
478   One may notice that in the construction of the stopping time, we set the threshold for the squared
479   gradient norm to be O(1вҲ’ОІ2 ). As we set 1вҲ’ОІ2 = Оҳ(Оө4 ), the threshold is actually much smaller than
480   what we aim for, since our goal is to have вҲҘвҲҮf (wt )вҲҘ2 вүӨ Оө2 . Therefore, based on the stopping-time
481   technique, we canвҲҡ     actually show that Adam can converge with an optimal rate of O(ОөвҲ’4 ) when
                   2
482   1 вҲ’ ОІ2 = Оө , or 1/ T if expressed in terms of the iteration number T . To the best of our knowledge,
483   this is the first time that Adam has been shown to converge with an optimal rate under the condition
484   that 1 вҲ’ ОІ2 = в„Ұ(1/T ), which greatly enlarges the hyperparameter range. We show in Appendix
485   D.2 that based on this technique, we can show Adam is hyperparameter agnostic even under the
      (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


486
      6   C ONCLUSION
487
488
      In this paper, we have conducted a mathematical examination of the performance of the Adam
489   optimizer and SGDM within the context of non-uniform smoothness. Our convergence analysis
490   reveals that Adam exhibits a faster rate of convergence compared to SGDM under these conditions.
491   Moreover, we introduce a novel stopping time technique that demonstrates AdamвҖҷs capability to
492   achieve the existing lower bounds for convergence rates. This finding underscores the robustness of
493   Adam in complex optimization landscapes and contributes to a deeper understanding of its theoretical
494   properties.

1025   Lemma 9. Let f1 , f2 , f3 : R вҶ’ R satisfies (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition. Then f1 (x) + f2 (y) + f3 (z)
       satisfies (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition.

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1026
       Proof. If we consider a point (x1 , y1 , z1 ) within a ball centered at (x2 , y2 , z2 ) with radius 1/L1 , it
1027   follows that x1 is within a ball centered at x2 with the same radius. Thus, we have:
1028
1029
1030    |вҲҮf1 (x1 ) вҲ’ вҲҮf1 (x2 )| вүӨ (L0 + L1 |вҲҮf1 (x1 )|)|x1 вҲ’ x2 | вүӨ (L0 + L1 |вҲҮf (x1 , y1 , z1 )|)|x1 вҲ’ x2 |.
1031
1032   The last inequality holds because вҲҮf1 (x1 ) is one coordinate of вҲҮf (x1 , y1 , z1 ). Similarly, we can
1033   derive:
1034
1035                       |вҲҮf2 (y1 ) вҲ’ вҲҮf2 (y2 )| вүӨ (L0 + L1 |вҲҮf (x1 , y1 , z1 )|)|y1 вҲ’ y2 |,
1036
1037
1038                       |вҲҮf3 (z1 ) вҲ’ вҲҮf3 (z2 )| вүӨ (L0 + L1 |вҲҮf (x1 , y1 , z1 )|)|z1 вҲ’ z2 |.
1039
       Taking the squared sum of these inequalities confirms that f is indeed (L0 , L1 )-smooth.

2284
2285   D.2     P ROOF OF PARAMETER - AGNOSTIC ADAM
2286
2287   D.2.1     R ELATED WORKS ON PARAMETER AGNOSTIC OPTIMIZATION
2288
       Parameter-agnostic optimization. The term вҖқparameter-agnosticвҖқ implies that the optimizer is
2289
       capable of converging without the need for extensive hyperparameter tuning or detailed knowledge
2290   of the task characteristics. Designing parameter-agnostic or parameter-free optimizers is a significant
2291   challenge, as it can help avoid the extensive cost associated with hyperparameter search. Existing
2292   works on parameter-agnostic optimization can be categorized into several streams based on the
2293   settings they are predicated upon. In the deterministic offline setting, it is widely acknowledged that
2294   GD is not parameter-agnostic, even under an L-smooth condition Nesterov et al. (2018). However,
2295   this can be rectified by combining the GD with the Backtracking Line Search technique Armijo
2296   (1966). In the stochastic offline setting, under the L-smooth condition, multiple algorithms have
2297   been shown to be parameter-agnostic Yang et al. (2023); Ward et al. (2020); Faw et al. (2022); Wang
2298
       et al. (2023b); Cutkosky & Mehta (2020). More recently, HuМҲbler et al. (2023) demonstrated that
       Normalized-SGDM can be parameter-agnostic even under an (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition. In the
2299
       realm of online convex optimization, Orabona & PaМҒl (2016); Orabona & Tommasi (2017) have shown
2300
       there exist parameter-free algorithms achieving optimal dependence regarding not only the final error
2301   but also other problem specifics.
2302
2303   D.2.2    O UR RESULTS
2304                        вҲҡ
2305   As we select О· = 1/ T , choosing 1вҲ’ОІ2 = в„Ұ(1/T ) has the advantage that the update norm decreases
2306   with respect to T . This makes Adam parameter-agnostic under the (L0 , L1 )-smooth condition, as the
2307   update norm will eventually become smaller than L11 as T increases.
2308   Theorem 12. Let Assumptions 1 and 2 hold. Then, at the t-th iteration, setting О· = вҲҡ1t , ОІ2 = 1вҲ’ вҲҡ1
                                                                                                        4 3,

       we have that Algorithm 1 satisfies
